Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of these groups to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield. The presence of a bulky aromatic ring may hinder the effective coordination with the metal center. Examples: 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in variable yields, but when combined with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids, they tend to result in high yield. This may be due to the pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

**End of Rules**

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid
        ['[OX2H][C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 3: Sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[OX2][CX4][OX2][CX4][OX2]']  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7]:  # Rules predicting high yield
                        row.append(1)
                    else:  # Rules predicting low yield
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[$([CX3](=O)[OX2H1]),$([NX3;H2])]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['c1ccccc1C(=O)O'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid
        ['[OX2H][C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 3: Sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][N+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['CCCCCCCC'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['c1ccncc1C(=O)O'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine with carboxylic acid
        ['c1ccccc1C=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[OX2][CX4][OX2]']  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 6]:  # Rules predicting high yield
                        row.append(1)
                    else:  # Rules predicting low yield
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['c1ccccc1C(=O)O'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid
        ['[OX2H][C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[C@H]([OX2H])[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 3: Sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][N+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['CCCCCCCC'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['c1ccncc1C(=O)O'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine with carboxylic acid
        ['c1ccccc1C=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[OX2][CX4][OX2]']  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 6]:  # Rules predicting high yield
                        row.append(1)
                    else:  # Rules predicting low yield
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6 is significantly lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be effectively capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the model is not complex enough to capture the data's nuances. The rules may not be comprehensive or detailed enough to account for the variability in the dataset.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 2 often has a high positive SHAP value, suggesting it might be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 2 also frequently has a high negative SHAP value, indicating it might be underestimating the loading in other contexts.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The consistent discrepancy between predicted and experimental values indicates that the rules need refinement to better align predictions with actual outcomes.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights Rule 2 as a significant contributor to prediction errors. This rule, which involves modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group, may need to be re-evaluated or split into more specific sub-rules to better capture the nuances of different molecular structures.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 2:**
   - Given its significant impact on both overestimated and underestimated loadings, Rule 2 should be refined. Consider breaking it down into more specific sub-rules that account for different types of phenyl group interactions or additional functional groups that may alter its effect.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - Rules that consistently show high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings should be adjusted. For instance, Rule 5, which involves electron-withdrawing groups, might need to be expanded to consider the context of these groups within different molecular frameworks.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider incorporating additional features or rules that capture more complex interactions. This could involve exploring new molecular descriptors or leveraging domain knowledge to identify additional relevant chemical properties.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules that frequently contribute to prediction errors and test modifications to these rules in subsequent iterations to evaluate their impact on accuracy.

5. **Incorporate Additional Data:**
   - If possible, incorporate more diverse data into the training set to improve the model's ability to generalize across different types of molecules. This could help in capturing a wider range of chemical interactions and improve the robustness of the rules.

By focusing on these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the complexities of the data, potentially leading to improved accuracy and more reliable predictions.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of these groups to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the stability and reactivity of the catalyst. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield. The presence of a bulky aromatic ring may hinder the effective coordination with the metal center. Examples: 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in variable yields, but when combined with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids, they tend to result in high yield. This may be due to the pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

**End of Rules**

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][C;X4][C;X4]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl with carboxylic acid and methyl
        ['[OX2H][C;R5,R6][OX2H]'],  # Rule 3: Multiple hydroxyl groups in sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group and carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[OX2][C;X4][OX2][C;X4][OX2]'],  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
        ['[c][OX2H]'],  # Rule 10: Hydroxyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[CX4](F)(F)F'],  # Rule 11: Trifluoromethyl group
        ['[n][NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 12: Pyridine and amino group
        ['[c][nH]'],  # Rule 13: Indole group
        ['[c][CH2Br]'],  # Rule 14: Bromomethyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']  # Rule 15: Hydroxyl and carboxylic acid on phenyl
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7, 12, 13, 14, 15]:  # Rules with high prediction
                        row.append(1)
                    else:
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

** Start of Suggestions **

- ** Suggestion 1 **: For Rule 1, the SMARTS pattern `['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]']` is correct for identifying carboxylic acid and amino groups. However, ensure that both groups are present in the same molecule, as the current implementation may not enforce this.

- ** Suggestion 2 **: For Rule 2, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][C;X4][C;X4]']` should be revised. The pattern should ensure that the phenyl group is directly attached to the carboxylic acid and that there is an electron-donating group like methyl. Consider using a pattern like `['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][CH3]']`.

- ** Suggestion 3 **: For Rule 3, the SMARTS pattern `['[OX2H][C;R5,R6][OX2H]']` may not fully capture sugar-like structures. Consider using a more specific pattern that captures multiple hydroxyl groups in a cyclic structure, such as `['[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])']`.

- ** Suggestion 4 **: For Rule 4, the SMARTS pattern `['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']` is generally correct, but ensure that the sulfur group is appropriately matched with the carboxylic acid in the same molecule.

- ** Suggestion 5 **: For Rule 5, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]']` correctly identifies nitro groups on aromatic rings. Ensure that the aromatic context is correctly captured.

- ** Suggestion 6 **: For Rule 6, the SMARTS pattern `['[C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4]']` should be revised to ensure it captures long aliphatic chains more effectively. Consider using a pattern like `['[C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4]']` to capture longer chains.

- ** Suggestion 7 **: For Rule 7, the SMARTS pattern `['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']` is correct for identifying pyridine with carboxylic acid. Ensure the pyridine ring is correctly matched.

- ** Suggestion 8 **: For Rule 8, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][CX3H]=O']` is correct for identifying formyl groups on aromatic rings. Ensure the aromatic context is correctly captured.

- ** Suggestion 9 **: For Rule 9, the SMARTS pattern `['[OX2][C;X4][OX2][C;X4][OX2]']` should be revised to better capture multiple ether linkages. Consider using a pattern like `['[OX2][C;X4][OX2][C;X4][OX2][C;X4]']`.

- ** Suggestion 10 **: For Rule 10, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][OX2H]']` is correct for identifying hydroxyl groups on aromatic rings. Ensure the aromatic context is correctly captured.

- ** Suggestion 11 **: For Rule 11, the SMARTS pattern `['[CX4](F)(F)F']` is correct for identifying trifluoromethyl groups. Ensure the context of the aromatic ring is considered if necessary.

- ** Suggestion 12 **: For Rule 12, the SMARTS pattern `['[n][NX3;H2]']` should be revised to ensure it captures pyridine with an amino group. Consider using a pattern like `['[n][NX3;H2]']` with additional context for the pyridine ring.

- ** Suggestion 13 **: For Rule 13, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][nH]']` is correct for identifying indole groups. Ensure the aromatic context is correctly captured.

- ** Suggestion 14 **: For Rule 14, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][CH2Br]']` is correct for identifying bromomethyl groups on aromatic rings. Ensure the aromatic context is correctly captured.

- ** Suggestion 15 **: For Rule 15, the SMARTS pattern `['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']` should be revised to ensure it captures hydroxyl and carboxylic acid groups on phenyl rings. Consider using a pattern like `['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']` with additional context for the phenyl ring.

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][CH3]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl with carboxylic acid and methyl
        ['[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])[C@H]([OH])'],  # Rule 3: Multiple hydroxyl groups in sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group and carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4][C;X4]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[OX2][C;X4][OX2][C;X4][OX2][C;X4]'],  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
        ['[c][OX2H]'],  # Rule 10: Hydroxyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[CX4](F)(F)F'],  # Rule 11: Trifluoromethyl group
        ['[n][NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 12: Pyridine and amino group
        ['[c][nH]'],  # Rule 13: Indole group
        ['[c][CH2Br]'],  # Rule 14: Bromomethyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']  # Rule 15: Hydroxyl and carboxylic acid on phenyl
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7, 12, 13, 14, 15]:  # Rules with high prediction
                        row.append(1)
                    else:
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

Current rules:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Metric Commenter Message:
Based on the current metrics and comparing them to the previous metrics, here are some observations and comments on the performance of the current rules:

1. **Train Support**: The train support has decreased from 0.0603 in the previous iteration to 0.0305 in the current iteration. This indicates that the proportion of records for which the entire rule is true has decreased. While this might suggest a more specific rule set, it could also mean that the rules are becoming too narrow and not capturing enough of the dataset.

2. **Train Confidence**: The train confidence has slightly decreased from 0.6311 to 0.6136. This suggests a minor reduction in the accuracy of the rules in predicting the outcome when the conditions are met. Although the decrease is not substantial, it indicates that the rules might be losing some predictive power.

3. **Train Lift**: The train lift has decreased from 1.2675 to 1.2287. Lift measures how much more likely the outcome is given the rule conditions compared to the general population. A lift greater than 1 is still good, indicating that the rules are providing useful information, but the decrease suggests that the rules are becoming less impactful in distinguishing the outcome compared to the baseline probability.

4. **Train Leverage**: The train leverage has decreased from 0.0076 to 0.0060. Leverage indicates the additional support the rule receives over what would be expected by chance. The decrease in leverage suggests that the rules are providing less additional information than before, which could mean that the rules are becoming less effective at identifying non-random associations.

### Recommendations for Improvement:

- **Broaden Rule Conditions**: Consider revisiting the rule conditions to ensure they are not too specific. Broader conditions might help increase support and confidence by capturing more relevant data points.

- **Refine Rule Definitions**: Analyze the current rules to identify any that might be too restrictive or not contributing significantly to the overall model. Simplifying or combining similar rules could help improve performance.

- **Focus on High-Impact Modifiers**: Prioritize rules that involve chemical groups or structures known to have a significant impact on catalytic performance. This could help improve lift and leverage by focusing on more influential factors.

- **Iterate and Validate**: Continue iterating on the rules, using cross-validation to ensure that changes lead to improvements in both training and test metrics. This will help avoid overfitting and ensure that the rules generalize well to new data.

Overall, while the current rules are still providing useful insights (as indicated by a lift greater than 1), there is room for improvement to enhance their predictive power and applicability.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules seem to be struggling with effectiveness, as indicated by the drop in validation accuracy from previous iterations (0.76) to the current iteration (0.5714). This suggests that the rules may not be capturing the underlying patterns in the data effectively.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The significant drop in validation accuracy suggests potential underfitting. The model may not be complex enough to capture the nuances of the data, or the rules may not be comprehensive enough to cover the variability in the dataset.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values (e.g., Rule 1, Rule 2, Rule 8) may be problematic. These rules might be overestimating the impact of certain features.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values (e.g., Rule 1, Rule 5, Rule 13) may be underestimating the impact of certain features.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The drop in accuracy indicates that the current rules may not be capturing the complexity of the data, and there is potential for refining the rules to improve performance.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules may be contributing to incorrect predictions. For instance, Rule 1 and Rule 2 often have high positive SHAP values for overestimated loadings, suggesting these rules might be too aggressive in predicting high loading.
   - Rules with consistently high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings, such as Rule 5 and Rule 13, may need to be adjusted to better capture the conditions that lead to high experimental loadings.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - Re-evaluate Rule 1 and Rule 2, which often contribute to overestimated loadings. Consider adding conditions or constraints to these rules to prevent overestimation.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - Modify Rule 5 and Rule 13 to better account for scenarios where the experimental loading is high. This could involve relaxing some constraints or incorporating additional features that might influence high loading.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider expanding the feature set or introducing new rules that capture additional chemical properties or interactions that are currently not considered.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. For example, if a rule consistently shows a high SHAP value for incorrect predictions, investigate the underlying assumptions of that rule and adjust accordingly.

5. **Incorporate Additional Data:**
   - If possible, incorporate more diverse data to help the model learn a broader range of patterns, which could improve generalization and reduce the risk of underfitting.

By focusing on these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the complexities of the data, potentially leading to improved accuracy and more reliable predictions.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][C]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid and electron-donating group
        ['[OX2H][C][C][C][C][C][OX2H]'],  # Rule 3: Multiple hydroxyl groups in sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group and carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][C][C][C][C][C][C][C]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine ring with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][OX2][C][OX2][C]'],  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
        ['[c][OX2H]'],  # Rule 10: Hydroxyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[CX4](F)(F)F'],  # Rule 11: Trifluoromethyl group
        ['[n][NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 12: Pyridine ring with amino group
        ['[c][nH]'],  # Rule 13: Indole group
        ['[c][CH2Br]'],  # Rule 14: Bromomethyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H]']  # Rule 15: Hydroxyl and carboxylic acid on phenyl ring
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7, 12, 13, 14, 15]:  # Rules predicting high yield
                        row.append(1)
                    else:  # Rules predicting low yield
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

    rules = [

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][C]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid and electron-donating group
        ['[OX2H][C][C][C][C][C][OX2H]'],  # Rule 3: Multiple hydroxyl groups in sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group and carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][C][C][C][C][C][C][C]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine ring with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][OX2][C][OX2][C]'],  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
        ['[c][OX2H]'],  # Rule 10: Hydroxyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[CX4](F)(F)F'],  # Rule 11: Trifluoromethyl group
        ['[n][NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 12: Pyridine ring with amino group
        ['[c][nH]'],  # Rule 13: Indole group
        ['[c][CH2Br]'],  # Rule 14: Bromomethyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H]']  # Rule 15: Hydroxyl and carboxylic acid on phenyl ring
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 2, 3, 4, 7, 12, 13, 14]:  # Rules predicting high yield
                        row.append(1)
                    else:  # Rules predicting low yield
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

Current rules:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

3. **Leverage**: The current train leverage is 0.0057, which is lower than the previous iteration's leverage values of 0.0076 and 0.0060. - **Balancing Specificity and Generality**: Strive to balance the specificity and generality of the rules. Highly specific rules may have high confidence but low support, while overly general rules may have high support but low confidence. Finding a middle ground could improve overall performance.

- **Iterative Testing and Validation**: Continue iterating on the rules and validate them against test data to ensure that improvements in training metrics translate to better generalization and performance on unseen data.

Overall, while the current rules show reasonable performance, there is room for improvement, particularly in enhancing the confidence and lift metrics to ensure the rules are both reliable and effective in making predictions.

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

#### 1. Evaluate Effectiveness:
The current rules have shown some improvement in validation accuracy, moving from 0.5714 to 0.6571. This suggests that the rules are becoming more effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data. However, the accuracy is still below the initial reference accuracies, indicating room for further improvement.

#### 2. Assess Overfitting/Underfitting:
The increase in validation accuracy suggests that the model is not overfitting to the training data. However, the relatively low accuracy compared to the reference metrics indicates potential underfitting, where the model may not be capturing all the relevant features or interactions.

#### 3. Analyze Discrepancies:
- **Overestimated Loadings**: For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values should be scrutinized. For example, Rule 3 and Rule 8 often have high positive SHAP values in such cases, suggesting they might be contributing to overestimation.
- **Underestimated Loadings**: For molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values should be examined. Rule 13 frequently shows negative SHAP values in these cases, indicating it might be contributing to underestimation.

#### 4. Interpret Accuracy Improvements:
The improvement in validation accuracy suggests that the current rules are moving in the right direction. However, the accuracy is still not at the level of the reference metrics, indicating that further refinement is needed to reach a local minimum.

#### 5. Derive Insights from SHAP Analysis:
- **Rule 3**: This rule often has high positive SHAP values for overestimated loadings. It might be too broadly defined, capturing cases where multiple hydroxyl groups do not necessarily lead to high yield.
- **Rule 8**: This rule also shows high positive SHAP values in overestimated cases. The formyl group's impact might be more context-dependent than currently accounted for.
- **Rule 13**: This rule shows high negative SHAP values in underestimated cases. The indole group's influence might be more significant than currently captured, suggesting a need for refinement.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 3**: Consider adding more specific conditions or exceptions to Rule 3 to prevent overestimation. For instance, the presence of other functional groups or steric hindrance might affect the rule's applicability.

2. **Adjust Rule 8**: Re-evaluate the impact of the formyl group in different molecular contexts. It might be beneficial to incorporate additional factors that influence the formyl group's effect on yield.

3. **Enhance Rule 13**: Given its frequent negative SHAP values in underestimated cases, Rule 13 could be expanded to include additional interactions or conditions that enhance the indole group's positive impact.

4. **Generalization Improvements**: To address potential underfitting, consider incorporating additional features or interactions that capture more complex relationships in the data. This could involve exploring new functional groups or molecular structures not currently covered by the rules.

5. **Iterative Refinement**: Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with consistently high SHAP values (positive or negative) and adjust them to better align with experimental outcomes.

By implementing these recommendations, the rules can be further refined to improve both the accuracy and generalization of the model, ultimately leading to better alignment with experimental data.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][C]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid and electron-donating group
        ['[OX2H][C][C][C][C][C][OX2H]'],  # Rule 3: Multiple hydroxyl groups in sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group and carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][C][C][C][C][C][C][C]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine ring with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][O][C][O][C]'],  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
        ['[c][OX2H]'],  # Rule 10: Hydroxyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[CX4](F)(F)F'],  # Rule 11: Trifluoromethyl group
        ['[n][NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 12: Pyridine ring and amino group
        ['[c][nH]'],  # Rule 13: Indole group
        ['[c][CH2Br]'],  # Rule 14: Bromomethyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']  # Rule 15: Hydroxyl group on phenyl ring and carboxylic acid
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 3, 4, 6, 12, 13, 14]:  # Rules with high prediction
                        row.append(1)
                    else:
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of this group may destabilize the catalyst interaction. Example: 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an amino group tend to result in high yield. The combination of pyridine's coordination ability and the amino group's potential for hydrogen bonding may enhance catalyst performance. Example: 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in high yield. The indole's aromaticity and potential for -stacking interactions may stabilize the catalyst. Example: (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a bromomethyl group attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The bromine atom may enhance coordination through halogen bonding. Example: 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring and an additional carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The combination of hydrogen bonding and potential coordination sites may stabilize the catalyst. Example: 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Code for rules:
def rule2matrix(smiles_list):
    rules = [
        ['[CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 1: Carboxylic acid and amino group
        ['[c][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]', '[c][C]'],  # Rule 2: Phenyl group with carboxylic acid and electron-donating group
        ['[OX2H][C][C][C][C][C][OX2H]'],  # Rule 3: Multiple hydroxyl groups in sugar-like structure
        ['[SX2][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 4: Sulfur-containing group and carboxylic acid
        ['[c][NX3+](=O)[O-]'],  # Rule 5: Nitro group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][C][C][C][C][C][C][C]'],  # Rule 6: Long aliphatic chains
        ['[n][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]'],  # Rule 7: Pyridine ring with carboxylic acid
        ['[c][CX3H]=O'],  # Rule 8: Formyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[C][O][C][O][C]'],  # Rule 9: Multiple ether linkages
        ['[c][OX2H]'],  # Rule 10: Hydroxyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[CX4](F)(F)F'],  # Rule 11: Trifluoromethyl group
        ['[n][NX3;H2]'],  # Rule 12: Pyridine ring and amino group
        ['[c][nH]'],  # Rule 13: Indole group
        ['[c][CH2Br]'],  # Rule 14: Bromomethyl group on aromatic ring
        ['[c][OX2H][CX3](=O)[OX2H1]']  # Rule 15: Hydroxyl group on phenyl ring and carboxylic acid
    ]
    results = []
    for smi in smiles_list:
        mol = Chem.MolFromSmiles(smi)
        if mol is None:
            results.append([0] * len(rules))
            continue
        row = []
        for i, rule in enumerate(rules):
            try:
                if all(mol.HasSubstructMatch(Chem.MolFromSmarts(r)) for r in rule):
                    if i in [0, 3, 4, 6, 12, 13, 14]:  # Rules with high prediction
                        row.append(1)
                    else:
                        row.append(-1)
                else:
                    row.append(0)
            except:
                row.append(0)
        results.append(row)
    df = pd.DataFrame(results, columns=[f'Rule {i+1}' for i in range(len(rules))])
    return df

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in high yield. This combination likely enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst stability and reactivity. Examples: (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group and an electron-donating group (e.g., methyl) tend to result in low yield. The steric hindrance and electronic effects may hinder effective coordination. Examples: 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in high yield. These groups can form multiple hydrogen bonds, potentially stabilizing the catalyst structure. Example: (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group (e.g., sulfanyl) and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The sulfur atom may provide additional coordination sites or influence the electronic properties of the catalyst. Examples: 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. These groups can alter the electronic environment, potentially enhancing the catalytic activity. Example: 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with long aliphatic chains (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The hydrophobic nature and steric bulk of long chains may interfere with effective catalyst interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional functional groups like carboxylic acids tend to result in high yield. The pyridine's ability to coordinate with metal centers may enhance reactivity. Example: pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in low yield. The formyl group may not provide effective coordination or stabilization. Examples: 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., polyether chains) tend to result in low yield. The flexibility and potential steric hindrance of these chains may reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. Example: 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group on an aromatic ring, without additional electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in low yield. The hydroxyl group alone may not provide sufficient coordination or electronic influence. Example: 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.
